<span id="freeTextContainer13492642921909140178">Under the streets of London there's a place most people could never even dream of. A city of monsters and saints, murderers and angels, knights in armour and pale girls in black velvet. This is the city of the people who have fallen between the cracks.<br /><br />Richard Mayhew, a young businessman, is going to find out more than enough about this other London. A single act of kindnes</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13492642921909140178">Under the streets of London there's a place most people could never even dream of. A city of monsters and saints, murderers and angels, knights in armour and pale girls in black velvet. This is the city of the people who have fallen between the cracks.<br /><br />Richard Mayhew, a young businessman, is going to find out more than enough about this other London. A single act of kindness catapults him out of his workday existence and into a world that is at once eerily familiar and utterly bizarre. And a strange destiny awaits him down here, beneath his native city: <em>neverwhere</em>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13492642921909140178">...more</a>

<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/19393388-cookiemonger" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1451368540i/19393388._UX100_CR0,5,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>

<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/19393388-cookiemonger">cookiemonger</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The author's preferred text is lightly revised (more like added to, really) and also available in audio. I don't think the differences are major, but<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The author's preferred text is lightly revised (more like added to, really) and also available in audio. I don't think the differences are major, but the many small additions to the text are quite good, in my opinion. Not to the point that you should read both editions, but I would recommend seeking out the preferred text.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23526342">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23526342" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/827856-steve" class="left imgcol" title="Steve"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1255806840p2/827856.jpg" alt="Steve" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23526342?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/827856-steve" class="user" title="Steve">Steve</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23526342">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7477401206579405361">I didn't enjoy this book as much as I should have. I kept distracting myself with the thought, "Why the hell haven't I read more of Neil Gaiman's books?" Then I would have to tell myself to shut up, because I'm reading one right now, and I can return to berating myself later.<br /><br />Neil Gaiman really understands fantasy. He understands that it isn't elves dancing in a forest and drunk dwarves mining for gold with improbably well maintained beards. No, fantasy is a reflection of reality, but fantastic.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7477401206579405361">I didn't enjoy this book as much as I should have. I kept distracting myself with the thought, "Why the hell haven't I read more of Neil Gaiman's books?" Then I would have to tell myself to shut up, because I'm reading one right now, and I can return to berating myself later.<br /><br />Neil Gaiman really understands fantasy. He understands that it isn't elves dancing in a forest and drunk dwarves mining for gold with improbably well maintained beards. No, fantasy is a reflection of reality, but fantastic. It isn't an alternate reality, but reality through the lens of imagination, and possibly some mind altering substances. <br /><br />Not only does he capture the essence of fantasy perfectly, but he does so with the trickiness and charming language that seems to be uniquely in the realm of British humorists. He rarely describes anything in a completely straightforward manner, instead choosing to almost fool the reader into visualizing his delightful (or, when appropriate, very undelightful) characters and events. <br /><br />I couldn't help but to be completely charmed by and immersed into London Below, right up until the point when some jackass started to wonder, "Why the hell haven't I read more of Neil Gaiman's books?"<br /><br />==============<br />So, a few years later, and a lot of people seem to like this review, still. I've decided to abuse the popularity of this review to attempt to steer fans of Neverwhere towards a few other books that I think they will enjoy. Despite the theme of the review being "Read more Neil Gaiman", the books listed below are actually by different authors, since I <strong>think</strong> I've already made it clear that you should read more Neil Gaiman. So, aside from all of those, here are a few other recommendations:<br /><br />1. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13432135">Bridge of Birds, by Barry Hughart</a> - An epic, hilarious mythological adventure.<br /><br />2.<a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/455041385">Wool, by Hugh Howey</a> - A pretty dark and serious tale of people living underground on a ruined world.<br /><br />3. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34053821">The Baroque Cycle, by Neal Stephenson</a> - A globetrotting adventure set among a lovingly researched historical backdrop. <br /><br />4. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74404442">Little Brother, by Cory Doctorow</a> - A nerd finds himself suddenly and unexpectedly being stalked by his own government, and doesn't think it's so cool.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7477401206579405361">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_101995601">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101995601" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" alt="Nataliya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/101995601?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads" class="actionLinkLite">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=i-also-saw-the-film" class="actionLinkLite">i-also-saw-the-film</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=location-is-the-true-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">location-is-the-true-protagonist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2015-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2015-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer101995601">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5026886293848457688"><em>Neverwhere</em> was my first real introduction to the world of urban fantasy - a clever take on <em>Alice in Wonderland</em>, one can say, set in the semi-magical, unsubtly dangerous, and quite fantastically warped world of 'London Below'.<blockquote>"<em>Young man," he said, "understand this: there are two Londons. There's London Above - that's where you lived - and then there's London Below - the Underside - inhabited by the people who fell through the cracks in the world. Now you're one of them. Good night.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694025._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><em>Neverwhere</em> is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5026886293848457688"><em>Neverwhere</em> was my first real introduction to the world of urban fantasy - a clever take on <em>Alice in Wonderland</em>, one can say, set in the semi-magical, unsubtly dangerous, and quite fantastically warped world of 'London Below'.<blockquote>"<em>Young man," he said, "understand this: there are two Londons. There's London Above - that's where you lived - and then there's London Below - the Underside - inhabited by the people who fell through the cracks in the world. Now you're one of them. Good night.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694025._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><em>Neverwhere</em> is one of my all-time favorite books. It has just the right amount of wild imagination (inexplicably somehow still grounded in firm reality), a healthy dose of absurdity and strangeness, remarkably colorful larger-than-life characters, unforgettable setting that is more of a character than a simple backdrop, and, of course, sufficient amount of lovely dry humor. <br /><br />Besides, there is that certain 'something' in Neil Gaiman's writing that keeps bringing me back to his works - that cleverness, I guess, that boldness in his approach to writing, that apt descriptiveness that burns scenes into your mind without becoming boringly detailed or repetitive, and the bit of mesmerizing darkness he harbors in all of his works, regardless of the theme or target audience.<br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694026._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />In <em>Neverwhere</em> Gaiman uses the old technique of taking a person belonging to the 'regular' world and throwing them into the midst of a fantastical reality, using the protagonist as our eyes into this world - think of "Alice in Wonderland" or "The Wizard of Oz" or its younger siblings like Miéville's "Un Lun Dun".<br /><br /><strong>Richard Mayhew is a timid and perfectly average London guy who suffers from a noticeable lack of figurative backbone</strong> - and the only time in his life he does show some of that ill-fated backbone, combined with some very real compassion, he gets himself into trouble that is waaaay over his head. Let Richard tell you about it himself:<blockquote>
  <em>"Dear Diary," he began. "On Friday I had a job, a fiancée, a home, and a life that made sense. (Well, as much as any life makes sense.) Then I found an injured girl bleeding on the pavement, and I tried to be a Good Samaritan. Now I've got no fiancée, no home, no job, and I'm walking around a couple of hundred feet under the streets of London with the projected life expectancy of a suicidal fruitfly."</em>
</blockquote>Unwittinly having thwarted an assassination attempt on a young girl named Door and having helped her because, let's face it, he's basically a decent guy, Richard suddenly finds himself in the <strong>London Below - a place for those who no longer belong to the regular 'London Above', a place for those who have slipped through the cracks of ordinary reality.</strong> It is a place that exists outside of our conventions of time and space, touching our reality but not quite overlapping it.<blockquote>"<em>There are little pockets of old time in London, where things and places stay the same, like bubbles in amber,” she explained. “There’s a lot of time in London, and it has to go somewhere—it doesn’t all get used up at once."<br />“I may still be hung over,” sighed Richard. “That almost made sense."</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694027._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>Just like London Above, London Below is a melting pot - except for this one is for relics and discards and misfits of all kinds of times and legends and beliefs and tales.</strong> It is a place for those who fell between the cracks - <em>think of those you usually do not notice even if you walk past them on the street, like the homeless, for instance; they are already invisible to us</em>. It is also a place for those who spent too much time in the company of the supernatural, and for those who don't fit in our world. <br /><br />You can view it as a colorful tapestry - or more aptly, as a dirty filthy rag made of mismatched and threadbare bits and pieces that once were something grand and even now create a mesmerizing albeit puzzling effect through their sheer strangeness and unexpected combination.<br /><br /><strong>And it is a place that has teeth and is ready to bite.</strong> It is harsh and cruel, full of menacing dangers lurking around every corner. Friends can quickly turn into foes, and promising a favor is a serious thing that can get you far in this world. It takes skill to survive here.<br /><br /><strong>And Richard is very much NOT prepared for that.</strong><blockquote>"<em>His life so far, he decided, had prepared him perfectly for a job in Securities, for shopping at the supermarket, for watching soccer on the television on the weekends, for turning up the thermostat if he got cold. It had magnificently failed to prepare him for a life as an un-person on the roofs and in the sewers of London, for a life in the cold and the wet and the dark.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694028._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />London Below is a place populated with creatures that have enough color and flair to them to easily stand out against the drab background of life and their surroundings. Of course I'm talking about Marquis here, the not-so-honest and yet brave and loyal (for a reasonable price of a favor) <strong>Marquis de Carabas</strong>, the guy who you would ultimately want covering your back in a sticky situation (as long as you can overlook the fact that he may have had something to do with creating the said sticky situation in the first place!)<blockquote>
  <em>"He..." Richard began. "The marquis. Well, you know, to be honest, he seems a little bit dodgy to me."<br />Door stopped. The steps dead-ended in a rough brick wall. "Mm," she agreed. "He's a little bit dodgy in the same way that rats are a little bit covered in fur."</em>
</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694029._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />With characters like Marquis, or the enigmatic single-minded Hunter, or pretty much anyone Richard comes in contact with in this weird mixture of rooftops and sewers and underground tunnels, <strong>London Below is a confusing blur to Richard's tired and overwhelmed senses.</strong> Some things don't make sense. Others make way too much sense, are way too literal (Earl's Court or Knightsbridge, for instance). He is so out of place here, it's almost painful to read, as you wince and cringe at his pathetic attempts to clutch to what he continues to view as safety and sanity. And no, this sanity does not normally involve girls named Door or a charming scoundrel Marquis de Carabas, or Angel Islington, or outwordly hired thugs Croup and Vandemar, or Rat Speakers, or Beast of London, or the real Old Bailey, or the nonexistent British Museum underground station where Earl's Court can get you if you so please. <blockquote>
  <em>"Richard did not believe in angels, he never had. He was damned if he was going to start now. Still, it was much easier not to believe in something when it was not actually looking directly at you and saying your name."</em>
</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694030.jpg" /><br /><br />But eventually Richard may, just may, start discovering something about himself that is a bit more adjusted in the world that has slipped through the cracks than "real" London. <strong>In other words, Richard Mayhew just may have gone native.</strong> Unlike Dorothy and her Toto, he may not want to just live happily ever after in his version of Kansas.<blockquote>"<em>Metaphors failed him, then. He had gone beyond the world of metaphor and simile into the place of things that *are*, and it was changing him.</em>"</blockquote><strong>After all, haven't we all at some point asked a question about what is the meaning of all that we are doing?</strong> Haven't we always wondered whether there ay be something else we may be better suited to be and do? Haven't we wanted to escape somewhere... well... else? Different? Isn't that some of the reasons we wan t to immerse ourselves into worlds of fantasy at least for a short while?<blockquote>
  <em>"Work. Home. The pub. Meeting girls. Living in the city. Life. Is that all there is?"</em>
</blockquote>Usually, however, the point is to return home, enriched by experiences of the outwordliness, and, of course, gain some appreciation for the life we used to take for granted before. Because, of course, no matter how much we want to, we will never escape the real life. Or can we? After reading this book, I know I would want to, had I been in Richard's place. But I cannot, and so I appreciate my mundane uneventful life - but what if I didn't have to? Who knows...<br /><br />.............<img width="300" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334749i/694031._SX540_.jpg" />........<blockquote>"<em>The marquis de Carabas raised an eyebrow. "Well?" he said, irritably. "Are you coming?"<br /><br />Richard stared at him for a heartbeat.<br /><br />Then Richard nodded, without trusting himself to speak, and stood up. And they walked away together through the hole in the wall, back into the darkness, leaving nothing behind them; not even the doorway.</em>"</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5026886293848457688">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_103250817">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103250817" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3270188-kat-kennedy" class="left imgcol" title="Kat Kennedy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394913263p2/3270188.jpg" alt="Kat Kennedy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/103250817?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 27, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3270188-kat-kennedy" class="user" title="Kat Kennedy">Kat Kennedy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3270188-kat-kennedy?shelf=kat-s-book-reviews" class="actionLinkLite">kat-s-book-reviews</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3270188-kat-kennedy?shelf=the-great-shelf-of-meh" class="actionLinkLite">the-great-shelf-of-meh</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer103250817">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13740692825112183135">I first started reading this book and honestly wanted to just chuck it in the bin. I said very mean things about the protagonist under my breath.<br /><br />Surely, I said, a Protagonist means that they are pro and totally into furthering the story. Surely, Protagonist is the similar to Proactive and Productive.<br /><br />I was wrong. The word Protagonist, in its basic form is not similar to proactive. It simply, from the Greek plays, means the principle character or the first speaking character.<br /><br />However, I maintain th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13740692825112183135">I first started reading this book and honestly wanted to just chuck it in the bin. I said very mean things about the protagonist under my breath.<br /><br />Surely, I said, a Protagonist means that they are pro and totally into furthering the story. Surely, Protagonist is the similar to Proactive and Productive.<br /><br />I was wrong. The word Protagonist, in its basic form is not similar to proactive. It simply, from the Greek plays, means the principle character or the first speaking character.<br /><br />However, I maintain that the kind of protagonist that most people want to read about is one that actually bloody does something!<br /><br />History/Language lesson over.<br /><br />Neverwhere is a book that TRIES to be clever and magical. In many senses it utterly manages to be magical and creative and fun. It fails, however, to be clever. There are so many lines in this book intending to be dry wit and just come off dry stupid. <br /><br />Allow an example:<br /><br /><em>There are four simple ways for the observant to tell Mr Coup and Mr Vandemar apart: first, Mr Vandemar is two and a half heads taller than Mr Croup; second, Mr Croup has eyes of a faded, china blue while Mr Vandemar's eyes are brown; third, while Mr Vandemar fashioned the rings he wears on his right hand out of the skulls of four ravens, Mr Croup has no obvious jewellery; fourth, Mr Croup likes words, while Mr Vandemar is always hungry. Also, they look nothing at all alike.</em> <br /><br />Oh! I see what you did there! *Kat laughs, slapping her knee with her hand in amusement* <br /><br />No, not really. If he'd left out these lame little lines I think I probably would have enjoyed this novel a whole lot more.<br /><br />Just about every other character in this story is awesome except for the protagonist. Give me a story about Marquis de Carabas and I will read it in a second. Honestly, fantastic character right there. Tell me I have to read another whiny missive about Richard Mayhew and I will likely stick hot pokers into my eyes first.<br /><br />I get it. I really do. It's a journey. He has to LEARN and GROW and CHANGE. But he takes a REALLY long time to do it and he only ever grows to be slightly less pansy, soft and annoying. The total character growth comes to equal someone who doesn't just sit idly by and let people take stuff from him.<br /><br />Let me give you an example. He met a girl who was unconscious in the streets and bleeding to death. He takes her home. This causes his fiancee to break up with him. He then goes through a lengthy process to get the girl back where she came from. Once he does this he then loses his job, his apartment and all his money. He then goes to find the girl for a) an explanation and b) help. Without her help he will probably die as another side effect of having met her is that he has two psychopathic killers on his tail. <br /><br />She simply apologizes and walks away, abandoning him. So what does he do? Does he chase her down and gently remind her that she owes him a favor? Does he barter and trade what he can, whilst trying to lure the killers into a trap so that he can some how defend himself? No. I will now transcribe from the book EXACTLY what he does.<br /><br /><em>Richard leaned against a wall, and listened to their footsteps, echoing away, and to the rush of the water running past on its way to the pumping station of East London, and the sewage works. "Shit," he said. And then, to his surprise, for the first time since his father died, alone in the dark, Richard Mayhew began to cry.</em> <br /><br />He decides to stay there and die. That's right, folks. He just stays there waiting to die. <br /><br />Boo-fucking-hoo.<br /><br />Ya know, I don't accept this crap from a female character - nor do I accept it from a man. How the hell am I suppose to sympathize with someone who so blithely lets everything he has slip through his fingers because he can't speak up and demand explanations or some kind of help? This level of pitiful doesn't help the audience empathize - it makes them think your protagonist is an idiot. <br /><br />The plot is pretty good - despite everything being painfully obvious and predictable at the end. <br /><br />The world building is fantastic. It's probably the best thing about this book. It's really creative and fascinating and interesting.<br /><br />Over all, it was an alright read. It wasn't great. I labored through until the last half where it began to pick up and markedly improve. Thus only three stars. Had the first half been more like the last half then it would have earned four.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13740692825112183135">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/759070661?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 08, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25375513-rick-riordan" class="user" title="Rick Riordan">Rick Riordan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer759070661">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4069856147925941817">Okay, so people have been telling me to read Neil Gaiman for ages. They assume I've read American Gods because the premise is similar to the Percy Jackson series. Well, I still haven't read American Gods, but I did pick up Neverwhere in the Heathrow airport and read it on the way back home. I enjoyed it a lot. Great fantasy, wonderful sense of humor. I can understand why Gaiman is so popular. I'll have to look up his other books.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_8142177">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8142177" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/139747-james" class="left imgcol" title="James"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1317341243p2/139747.jpg" alt="James" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/8142177?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 23, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/139747-james" class="user" title="James">James</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Kids</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/139747-james?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8142177">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8244072675182092416">Picture Hitch Hiker's Guide to the Galaxy. Really good books right? Now imagine if someone took the first book and mad libbed characters, settings, monsters, etc, so that you were left with the exact same story except instead of travelling the galaxy with your crazy friend and the most powerful man in the universe, you were travelling in haunted British sewers with a nerd's wet dream of a Xena warrior princess rival and a Gothic princess who can unlock doors (sigh). I don't think there is much o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8244072675182092416">Picture Hitch Hiker's Guide to the Galaxy. Really good books right? Now imagine if someone took the first book and mad libbed characters, settings, monsters, etc, so that you were left with the exact same story except instead of travelling the galaxy with your crazy friend and the most powerful man in the universe, you were travelling in haunted British sewers with a nerd's wet dream of a Xena warrior princess rival and a Gothic princess who can unlock doors (sigh). I don't think there is much of a coincidence either because Gaiman wrote the Hitchhiker's companion in the early 80s, as well as conducted interviews with Adams while he was alive (not to mention took writing tips from him). The only <a title="The Saving Graces A Novel by Patricia Gaffney" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/130916.The_Saving_Graces_A_Novel">saving grace</a> for me with this book was the fact that there someone actually made a miniseries on the BBC of it that is actually so bad that it makes the book look half way decent. Between all the cliche characters, cheesy narration, and formulaic plot, its just not worth your money or a read, especially when Hitch Hiker exists already. I've said it before, stick to American Gods and The Sandman when it comes to Gaiman.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8244072675182092416">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9612314165741499300">James, uh uh. No. Hitchhiker's Guide and Neverwhere are apples and oranges.               <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer424275539">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12736971928244587513"><strong>Dear Book</strong>,<br /><br />Why did you get over so soon?? Why did you get over at all?? :(<br /><br />I could have spent many more hours exploring those underground tunnels in the company of those wonderful people, and reveling in the amazing things that some of them - especially marquis de Carabas - had to say.<br /><br />I could have spent many more days reading and re-reading those delightfully formed sentences, laughing at the intelligent absurdity of it even when not-so-scary villains were doing not-so-pretty things.<br /><br />I could have s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12736971928244587513"><strong>Dear Book</strong>,<br /><br />Why did you get over so soon?? Why did you get over at all?? :(<br /><br />I could have spent many more hours exploring those underground tunnels in the company of those wonderful people, and reveling in the amazing things that some of them - especially marquis de Carabas - had to say.<br /><br />I could have spent many more days reading and re-reading those delightfully formed sentences, laughing at the intelligent absurdity of it even when not-so-scary villains were doing not-so-pretty things.<br /><br />I could have spent many more rainy Sundays cooped up in my room and dreaming about rat-speakers and angels, midnight markets and doors, the London Above and the London Below.<br /><br />But no matter how much I tried to prolong it (and trust me, I did), the inevitable happened.<br /><br />I made it past the last page.<br /><br /><u>And I miss you.</u> So very much :'(<br /><br />Why are you a standalone, book? Why don't you have a sequel?<br /><br />I loved you before I even started - maybe the words <em>'Neil Gaiman'</em> on your cover had something to do with that?? Three chapters later and I was unimpressed with your protagonist. Richard Mayhew was such a... such a... <em>damsel in distress</em> - except he wasn't a damsel (Come to think of it, what is the opposite of damsel???) Anyway, I mentally pegged you as a 4-star then but somewhere in those dank tunnels, you charmed me, book. You made me fall in love with you. You won me over with your ready wit and imagination and your lovely, lovely sense of humor.<br /><br />Thank you for coming into my life even if it was for a brief while. Thank you for taking me to that wonderful world. Maybe I'll fall through the cracks and go there someday. Or maybe I'll just revisit it in my dreams - safer that way.<br /><br />Love you. Miss you.<br /><br /><br />Your <s>slightly insane</s> reader,<br /><br />Scarlet.<br /><br /><br /><u>P.s.</u> Please tell Neil Gaiman to never, ever stop writing.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_771157942">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771157942" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/25353295-carmen" class="left imgcol" title="Carmen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1412617445p2/25353295.jpg" alt="Carmen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/771157942?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 07, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25353295-carmen" class="user" title="Carmen">Carmen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Carmen by: </span>
      Library
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=traditionally-published" class="actionLinkLite">traditionally-published</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer771157942">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11178303791383288254"><em>Richard had noticed that events were cowards: they didn't occur singly, but instead they would run in packs and leap out at him all at once.</em><br /><br />This is a very well-written and predictable book by Neil Gaiman.<br /><br />Richard is an office drone.<br /><br /><strong>Of course he is.</strong><br /><br />And he's dating a go-getting, beautiful woman who doesn't love him for what he is, but sees "potential" and dreams about the man she can turn him into. You know the type. Scarlett Johansson from DON JON.<br /><br />And he's miserable, but pretends he's happy becaus</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11178303791383288254"><em>Richard had noticed that events were cowards: they didn't occur singly, but instead they would run in packs and leap out at him all at once.</em><br /><br />This is a very well-written and predictable book by Neil Gaiman.<br /><br />Richard is an office drone.<br /><br /><strong>Of course he is.</strong><br /><br />And he's dating a go-getting, beautiful woman who doesn't love him for what he is, but sees "potential" and dreams about the man she can turn him into. You know the type. Scarlett Johansson from DON JON.<br /><br />And he's miserable, but pretends he's happy because... she's beautiful, I guess? Our first clue that he's a moron.<br /><br />And he's an orphan.<br /><br /><strong>Of course he is.</strong><br /><br />One day, which seemed just like every other ordinary day, he and Girlfriend are walking to an important dinner with Important People whom Girlfriend really really wants to impress, when Richard stumbles upon a filthy, skinny young woman in rags who is bleeding profusely. <br /><br />Girlfriend is not sympathetic but Richard shows mercy<br /><br /><strong>Of course he does</strong><br /><br />...and takes her back to his apartment where he tends to her wounds.<br /><br />She is not from this world, but from another world, a darker and more dangerous world, hidden right beneath London's surface....<br /><br /><strong>Of course. I know where this is going...</strong><br /><br />And she's some sort of princess or something....<br /><br /><strong>Of course...</strong><br />...<br /><br /><br />I am unsure if I have simply read too many books.<br /><br />Too many books, or too many books by the talented Mr. Gaiman, but either way I saw 99% of the plot coming from 10 miles off.<br /><br />Gaiman was constantly trying to shock me and surprise me. I was neither shocked nor surprised.<br /><br />And I'm starting to think that Gaiman has just one plot, a plot that he uses over and over and over and over and over and over and over and over and over and over again in each novel/story.<br /><br />I've gotten used to his preferences. Like hidden worlds full of sinister and quirky characters. Orphans. Evil bad guys who talk and dress as if they are Victorian gentleman and are always referred to in some respectful way.<br /><br /><br />I'm not slamming Gaiman. He's an excellent storyteller - excellent. The book was fun, quick, and had great world-building. If only I hadn't seen all the characters before. If only I hadn't heard all the details of the plot before. I was disappointed in how little Gaiman was able to surprise me or interest me here.<br /><br /><br />PLUSES:<br /><br /> - If it's your first Gaiman book, you'll probably be blown away.<br /><br /> - Wonderful rat characters who are cute and charming.<br /><br /> - Amazing world-building.<br /><br /> - On-point dialogue, fun characters, a breezy, action-adventure movie type feel with that special Gaiman dark-but-not-really-dark feel thrown in.<br /><br /> - Is able to maintain a "dark fairytale" feel while included things like the word "fuck" and used condom wrappers.<br /><br /> - Good ideas that he works into his writing, such as:<br /><br /><em>When he had first arrived, he had found London huge, odd, fundamentally incomprehensible, with only the Tube map, that elegant multicolored topographical display of underground railway lines and stations, giving it any semblance of order. Gradually he realized that the Tube map was a handy fiction that made life easier but bore no resemblance to the reality of the shape of the city above. It was like belonging to a political party, he thought once, proudly, and then, having tried to explain the resemblance between the Tube map and politics, at a party, to a cluster of bewildered strangers, he had decided in the future to leave political comment to others.</em><br /><br />Or what about this:<br /><br /><em>He continued, slowly, by a process of osmosis and white knowledge (which is like white noise, only more useful), to comprehend the city,...</em><br /><br />Isn't that interesting? It's such an interesting idea.<br /><br /> - Sometimes Gaiman is genuinely funny, usually when he's discussing sex.<br /><br /><em>The thin girl was gulping down one of Richard's bananas in what was, Richard reflected, the least erotic display of banana-eating he had ever seen.</em><br /><br />Or how about this, this cracked me up:<br /><br /><em>A late-night couple, who had been slowly walking along the Embankment toward them, holding hands, sat down in the middle of the bench, between Richard and Anaestheisa, and commenced to kiss each other, passionately. "Excuse me," said Richard to them. The man had his hand inside the woman's sweater and was moving it around enthusiastically, a lone traveler discovering an unexplored continent. "I want my life back," Richard told the couple.<br /><br />"I love you," said the man to the woman.<br /><br />"But your wife - "she said, licking the side of his face.<br /><br />"Fuck her," said the man.<br /><br />"Don' wanna fuck HER," said the woman, and she giggled, drunkenly. "Wanna fuck YOU..." She put a hand on his crotch and giggled some more.</em><br /><br />I found this very funny.<br /><br /><br /><br />MINUSES:<br /><br /> - Pathetic man-child hero who is supposed to go from zero to hero, but instead goes from zero to perhaps a zero-point-five. Starts off as a worthless noodle, ends up being a worthless potato. Slightly harder, more nutritious, but basically still brainless and not worth much.<br /><br /> - The book is very predictable.<br /><br /> - The book, being a "dark fairy tale" like the majority of Gaiman's books, can be a little cutesy and trite at times.<br /><br /> - Gaiman often tries too hard. I found his evil Victorian gentleman villains to be a LITTLE too dramatic and over-the-top for my tastes. It's just...<br /><br /><em>It was too consistent, to steady and inexorable a walk to be described as a stroll: Death walked like Mr. Vandemar.</em><br /><br />Oh, yes. He walked like death. Uh-huh. Please make some attempt to control yourself, Mr. Gaiman. I can handle the sharp teeth and long black coats and eating pigeons and all that other crap, but there has to be some end to the hyperbole, surely?<br /><br /> - Not only does he try too hard in the writing (occasionally) but some of the jokes are just... COME ON. We have De Carabas stealing candy from a baby at one point. LITERALLY. Stealing candy from a baby. Isn't that a bit much? I think it's a bit much. There's no other reason to do it than to show what kind of man De Carabas is.<br /><br />This is the kind of thing I'm dealing with here.<br /><br />Or "Mind the Gap." You know, that sign by the train that says, "Mind the gap"? And in this book the "gap" is actually this wraith-like thing that comes out of that space and tries to kill you or eat you or something.<br /><br />You know. Like Alice in Wonderland jokes. There's a lot of Alice in Wonderland type jokes in here.<br /><br />You should see how Gaiman takes paragraphs (PARAGRAPHS) to lovingly and carefully set up a "the penny has dropped" joke. It's... Well... Not too funny, IMO.<br /><br />Or what about when Gaiman has Richard go up to Jessica, and - to prove that he knows her - say, <br /><br /><em>"You're Jessica Bartram. You're a marketing executive at Stocktons. You're twenty-six. Your birthday is April the 23rd, and in the throes of extreme passion you have a tendency to hum the Monkees song "I'm a Believer"...."</em><br /><br />Listen, I see what Gaiman is trying to do. He thinks that a woman who hums "I'm a Believer" when she's having sex is funny. But the whole idea is ludicrous. If you are "in the throes of extreme passion" you should be unable to hum anything. Or, ideally, be unable to even form coherent thought. The whole idea is ridiculous to the point where it wasn't funny to me.<br /><br /><strong>You think THAT'S ridiculous in a book about beasts living in sewers and angels and vampires and etc.?</strong><br /><br />Yes.<br /><br /><strong>Okay. Weirdo.</strong><br /><br /> - The ending. I mean, SO PREDICTABLE. The <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[not-so-nice ex-girlfriend comes crawling back to the pathetic man-child and begs him to marry her. He's no longer interested. He's so far above her now. He can't even be tempted. (*Carmen is angry*) Typical male fantasy.<br /><br />He's returned to the real world after becoming The Warrior (who defeated the Beast and saved people blah blah blah) only to find out a 9-5 office job doesn't hold his interest anymore? There's a big surprise. He ends up deciding to go back and live in the filthy but magical Otherworld? There's a big surprise. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />But perhaps what upsets me most about the ending is that he's supposed to be transformed from a man-child or a pasty untested office drone or a slave to the Matrix or what-have-you into a strong, capable, competent man and I just DID NOT BUY this. I'm not buying it. Not to mention that I'm still EXTREMELY angry with Richard for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[letting that rat-girl Anaesthesia die.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Fucking hell, it was like he didn't even... I mean, fuck. He should have DONE SOMETHING. He just accepted <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[her death <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> like it was nothing. Am I supposed to be OKAY with this shit? Am I supposed to be okay that he didn't fight for her? No, I'm not okay with this. Fuck that.<br /><br /> - Oh, and he's a moron. Richard is a huge moron throughout the book. And he never gets any smarter. To my intense dismay. I find it hard to respect or admire someone so stupid.<br /><br />...<br /><br />Tl;dr - In conclusion: An excellent story wonderfully told by a master storyteller.<br /><br />Unfortunately, due to me having read too many books or perhaps too many books by this particular author, I was not as impressed as I had hoped to be.<br /><br />Still, a great dark fairy tale with rich and deep worldbuilding and fascinating ideas.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_699517517">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/699517517" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3145526-traveller" class="left imgcol" title="Traveller"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423246742p2/3145526.jpg" alt="Traveller" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/699517517?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3145526-traveller" class="user" title="Traveller">Traveller</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=three-and-a-half-stars" class="actionLinkLite">three-and-a-half-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer699517517">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2303349621738379219"><br /><em>*7/10 spangly shooting stars</em><br /><br />A contemporary urban fantasy novel in typical slightly off-beat Gaiman style. It's mainly a portal fantasy (the kind where you go from the "everyday" world to a "special" world through some kind of portal, as with <a title="Alice in Wonderland (Alice's Adventures in Wonderland #1) by Lewis Carroll" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13023.Alice_in_Wonderland__Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland__1_">Alice in Wonderland</a>, <a title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe / The Magician's Nephew by C.S. Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1797057.The_Lion__the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe___The_Magician_s_Nephew">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe / The Magician's Nephew</a>, <a title="Harry Potter Boxset (Harry Potter, #1-7) by J.K. Rowling" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/862041.Harry_Potter_Boxset__Harry_Potter___1_7_">Harry Potter Boxset</a>, <a title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz (Oz, #1) by L. Frank Baum" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/236093.The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz__Oz___1_">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a>, <a title="The Fionavar Tapestry (The Fionavar Tapestry #1-3) by Guy Gavriel Kay" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1148721.The_Fionavar_Tapestry__The_Fionavar_Tapestry__1_3_">The Fionavar Tapestry</a>, etc.). The 'alternate world' (the world where Londoners who "fall through the cracks" go), has the sett</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2303349621738379219"><br /><em>*7/10 spangly shooting stars</em><br /><br />A contemporary urban fantasy novel in typical slightly off-beat Gaiman style. It's mainly a portal fantasy (the kind where you go from the "everyday" world to a "special" world through some kind of portal, as with <a title="Alice in Wonderland (Alice's Adventures in Wonderland #1) by Lewis Carroll" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13023.Alice_in_Wonderland__Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland__1_">Alice in Wonderland</a>, <a title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe / The Magician's Nephew by C.S. Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1797057.The_Lion__the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe___The_Magician_s_Nephew">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe / The Magician's Nephew</a>, <a title="Harry Potter Boxset (Harry Potter, #1-7) by J.K. Rowling" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/862041.Harry_Potter_Boxset__Harry_Potter___1_7_">Harry Potter Boxset</a>, <a title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz (Oz, #1) by L. Frank Baum" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/236093.The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz__Oz___1_">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a>, <a title="The Fionavar Tapestry (The Fionavar Tapestry #1-3) by Guy Gavriel Kay" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1148721.The_Fionavar_Tapestry__The_Fionavar_Tapestry__1_3_">The Fionavar Tapestry</a>, etc.). The 'alternate world' (the world where Londoners who "fall through the cracks" go), has the setting and atmosphere of a <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaslamp_fantasy">Gaslamp fantasy</a>.<br /><br />I loved the eminently accessible style and the humor. The well-executed pace and tension kept me easily and continuously turning the pages - at some points even faster, due to good foreshadowing and tension-building, without me ever stalling my read out of boredom. The characterization (one couldn't help becoming fond of dear old bumbling Richard much as you do of Bilbo Baggins), was also good, so pretty much all of the basic literary elements were well executed. <br /><br />...and speaking of Bilbo Baggins, structurally speaking, the novel felt like a fairly standard monomyth, and also has elements of a Bildungsroman. (In fact, I had to keep stopping myself from mentally ticking off the 'stages' of the traditional fairy tale as per the structuralist narrative analysis of folk tales by Vladimir Propp and Svetlan Todorov.)<br />Is Gaiman doing this on purpose, though subtly twisting it? It seems awfully similar to one of his other works, <a title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Stardust</a> , from a narratology/structuralist point of view.<br /><br />..but the ending to some extent subtly thwarts the traditional folk narrative - it goes a bit farther, in that the hero might not quite end up where he had started. (Which is a good thing, of course - uniqueness in literature is always a good thing; we don't want to be reading cookie-cutter books all the time, do we?)<br /><br />I'm struggling to decide between a 3 and a 4 star rating. I had already rated most of the fantasy fare that I had enjoyed over the years 3 stars - (but we need more stars, Goodreads!) ..so giving this one more would, for instance put it above Lee and le Guin and on par with Gene Wolfe - and Wolfe is, in my opinion, definitely a cut above this - but then I should probably go and rate my Wolfe upwards....<br /><br /> I'm especially trying to decide how much I like the ending. Enough for a 4? Is Gaiman just patly endorsing escapism, a sort of Never-neverland of never growing up and not facing Life? ...or is he saying there is more to life than just eating, rutting, sleeping and earning the wherewithal to do so? It seems ambiguous enough to leave me pondering, and that is a good thing too!<br /><br />Is Neverwhere a metaphor for the "soul"? For creativity maybe, or perhaps for meaning? Is Gaiman saying we should stop to smell the flowers, to see the magic in Life? Is he saying that we should make a conscious effort to keep the magic - the wonder that a child feels for life - that thing that C.S. Lewis described as "joy", in our lives? As opposed to the other possible interpretation, being that this is simply a pat fantasy escapist novel which puts out the general implication that Life is too dull and boring to be bearable? If the former interpretation holds true, then I should be giving this 4 stars, which is my rating for more exceptional books. ...but the last chapter is so ambiguous... Hmmmmm. It's really hard, this star-rating thing.<br /><br />Well, maybe it deserves 4 stars just for the marvelous creation of the magnificent character of Hunter, who seemed to me that she could be a slightly more 'tribal' version of this: <img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1349949791p8/597237.jpg" /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2303349621738379219">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_50496586">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50496586" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja" class="left imgcol" title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/50496586?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja" class="user" title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">urban fantasy readers</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=quest" class="actionLinkLite">quest</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=angels" class="actionLinkLite">angels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=favorite-or-autobuy-author" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-or-autobuy-author</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=fish-out-of-water" class="actionLinkLite">fish-out-of-water</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=male-pov" class="actionLinkLite">male-pov</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=set-in-uk" class="actionLinkLite">set-in-uk</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=alternate-dimension-or-world" class="actionLinkLite">alternate-dimension-or-world</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=unlikely-hero-or-heroine" class="actionLinkLite">unlikely-hero-or-heroine</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=hero-i-liked" class="actionLinkLite">hero-i-liked</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=2011-reading" class="actionLinkLite">2011-reading</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=character-evolves-well-in-book" class="actionLinkLite">character-evolves-well-in-book</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=library-checkout" class="actionLinkLite">library-checkout</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=character-diversity" class="actionLinkLite">character-diversity</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=set-in-london" class="actionLinkLite">set-in-london</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=fa-top-100-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">fa-top-100-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer50496586">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4933324480614224267">Neverwhere is one of those books that answers the 'what if' question about the city in which you live your days, going through your admittedly 'mundane' routines. In this case, London. What if there was a London Below, a strange world which consists of Underground railways, tunnels, sewers, and sometimes uncannily parallels the London that the average inhabitant thought they knew but didn't, and in a way that seems a lot more interesting? It's a scary place, not terribly clean and sanitary. But</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4933324480614224267">Neverwhere is one of those books that answers the 'what if' question about the city in which you live your days, going through your admittedly 'mundane' routines. In this case, London. What if there was a London Below, a strange world which consists of Underground railways, tunnels, sewers, and sometimes uncannily parallels the London that the average inhabitant thought they knew but didn't, and in a way that seems a lot more interesting? It's a scary place, not terribly clean and sanitary. But it's also a place of wonders. If you spent some time there, would you find yourself, and realize that the normal world just doesn't hold the same charm for you, now that you've realized the possibilities? With this book, you can ponder these questions for yourself through the viewpoint of Richard Mayhew.<br /><br />The unknown is scary for us. Scary, but also exciting. You just have to find the courage to seek it out. That's one thing I love about books. They take me places that I'm not sure I'd want to go in real life. And in the process, they make me want to be braver than I am. Richard has to find that courage (the hard way), but he becomes a hero and a champion in his own right in the process of his journey. He faces pain, loss, and uncertainty, but he gains a lot more in return. <br /><br />This is the third book I've read by Neil Gaiman (all on audio), and I really appreciate his writing. He has a wonderful way with humor, a grand sense of adventure and whimsy, and he finds the uncanny and fantastical in the everyday and ordinary. Honestly, that's why I love fantasy, particularly urban fantasy. <br /><br />Although parts of this story were dark in subject matter and could have been too gruesome, the writing keeps the subject from being over the top in these areas. Subtlety in storytelling gives this reader enough to know just how bad the bad guys are, and without the scenes being too off-putting. And there is always hope that good will win out. I need that in a book. <br /><br />Of course, living in the sewer and the underground aren't the most clean ways to go about one's business, but there was also an undeniable appeal to these worlds. I'm not saying you will see me taking off on a sewer adventure (not going to happen), but at least I can read about it, and think that it didn't sound quite as bad as I thought it would (for the most part).<br /><br />I liked the diversity of this world. People of different colors, shapes, sizes and origins. That's how a big city like London truly is, not the monochromatic make-believe of some of the shows on TV (which I won't name) where you wonder how the characters can go seven seasons without ever encountering a person of color. And the diversity isn't just background filler. Diverse people have strong roles in this story. With this added appeal, it made the novel even more enjoyable.<br /><br />Neverwhere was a fun, interesting novel, with some mystical, otherworldly elements right smack dab in the middle of the everyday. I loved that about this book. I am so glad I started reading Neil Gaiman. I recommend you give this book a try if you haven't read him yet.<br /><br />Overall rating: <strong>4.25/5.0 stars</strong>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4933324480614224267">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_440327027">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440327027" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="left imgcol" title="Madeline"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" alt="Madeline" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/440327027?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="user" title="Madeline">Madeline</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Paperback 9780060557812" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer440327027">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16543550692055982584">God damn it, Goodreads, when are you going to let me use half-star ratings? You had enough time to make those hideous green "want to read" tabs next to every damn book in my newsfeed; could you please throw me a bone and stop confining me to full stars? I demand half stars, Goodreads. Particularly for this review, because three stars seems unfairly low, and four stars is a bit much. <br /><br /><em>Neverwhere</em> is early Gaiman (published in 1996), and it shows. You can see him working on the formula that would ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16543550692055982584">God damn it, Goodreads, when are you going to let me use half-star ratings? You had enough time to make those hideous green "want to read" tabs next to every damn book in my newsfeed; could you please throw me a bone and stop confining me to full stars? I demand half stars, Goodreads. Particularly for this review, because three stars seems unfairly low, and four stars is a bit much. <br /><br /><em>Neverwhere</em> is early Gaiman (published in 1996), and it shows. You can see him working on the formula that would make <em>American Gods</em>, <em>Anansi Boys</em>, and <em>Coraline</em> successes - unassuming Everyman gets sucked into a magical, dangerous new world and had to navigate through the insanity - but not quite getting it right. This reads sort of like a first draft of his later, more well-crafted books. <br /><br />The story takes place in modern-day London, and our Everyman is Richard Mayhew, who discovers a girl bleeding on the street one night. He takes her to his apartment and patches her up, and almost immediately a sinister pair of not-quite-men appear at Richard's apartment looking for the girl. The girl's name is Door, and the rest of her family was murdered. Door has the ability to open things that are locked (hence her name), and is being hunted because of this ability. Richard tags along with her, not entirely willingly, into London Below, which is a classic Gaiman world of magic and terror. And, in true Gaiman fashion, the fate of the world hangs in the balance.<br /><br />It's a good story, the characters and worldbuilding are magnificent, and it's full of Gaiman's signature reminders that magical worlds are actually fucking terrifying, as well as plenty of his unexpectedly funny bits, like this exchange:<br /><br />"The door was opened by a sleepy-looking footman, wearing a powdered, crooked wig and scarlet livery. He looked at the motley rabble on his doorstep with an expression that indicated that they had not been worth getting out of bed for.<br />'Can I help you?' said the footman. Richard had been told to fuck off and die with more warmth and good humor."<br /><br />But throughout the book, even as I was being alternately thrilled and horrified by the story, there was a palpable feeling that something was missing. The villains, while terrifying, were somehow not as threatening as they should have been; I saw several of the big plot twists coming a mile away <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Seriously though, was <em>anyone</em> surprised that Hunter turned out to be the traitor? And obviously the angel was the Big Bad, because the Marquis was too obvious and there weren't any other characters available for the role <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>; and somehow the big quest never felt important or developed enough. It was something involving a key - I read the book a few days ago, and already the details of the mission are slipping away - but I never felt like I really understood the importance of it all, and it just functioned as a way for the characters to keep hanging out and struggling against evil together. By the end of the book, the entire quest just felt like one big MacGuffin, and I was left feeling slightly deflated. <br /><br />None of this should suggest, of course, that this book isn't good. Because lest we forget, Average-Quality Gaiman is still Really Really Good Gaiman. Even after all the mild disappointments I listed, I still found myself invested in the world of London Below, and wishing that I could read more about it. Ultimately, the book functions as a good introduction to Gaiman and why we love him, but it doesn't hold up to his later works. <br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16543550692055982584">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10017804422949082268">I can't agree more with your review. I too gave Neverwhere a three, but I agree it feels low. For Gaiman, this book is a meh-average kind of story. Bu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10017804422949082268">I can't agree more with your review. I too gave Neverwhere a three, but I agree it feels low. For Gaiman, this book is a meh-average kind of story. But I get the feeling if you presented it to me (or anyone else familiar with Gaiman's other work) as the work of a different author, it'd be an easy four stars. I think you nailed it that this books deserves a 3.5 rating, and that number should be given with the "for a book by Neil Gaiman" disclaimer.</span>
